remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Onde_electromagnetique.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="176" data-file-width="714" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Onde_electromagnetique.svg/400px-Onde_electromagnetique.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Onde_electromagnetique.svg/600px-Onde_electromagnetique.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Onde_electromagnetique.svg/800px-Onde_electromagnetique.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Onde_electromagnetique.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linearly polarized</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinusoidal" title="Sinusoidal">sinusoidal</a> electromagnetic wave, propagating in the direction +<b>z</b> through a homogeneous, isotropic, dissipationless medium, such as vacuum. The electric field (<span style="color:blue;">blue</span> arrows) oscillates in the &#177;<b>x</b>-direction, and the orthogonal magnetic field (<span style="color:red;">red</span> arrows) oscillates in phase with the electric field, but in the &#177;<b>y</b>-direction.</div></div></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <b>electromagnetic radiation</b> (<b>EM radiation</b> or <b>EMR</b>) refers to the waves (or their <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quanta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a>, propagating (radiating) through space, carrying electromagnetic <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiant energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It includes <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwaves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">(visible) light</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Classical_electromagnetism" title="Classical electromagnetism">Classically</a>, electromagnetic radiation consists of <b>electromagnetic waves</b>, which are synchronized <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> that propagate at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, which, in a vacuum, is commonly denoted <i>c</i>. In homogeneous, isotropic media, the oscillations of the two fields are perpendicular to each other and perpendicular to the direction of energy and wave propagation, forming a <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse wave</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Wavefront" title="Wavefront">wavefront</a> of electromagnetic waves emitted from a <a href="/wiki/Point_source" title="Point source">point source</a> (such as a light bulb) is a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a>. The position of an electromagnetic wave within the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a> can be characterized by either its <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> of oscillation or its <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a>.  Electromagnetic waves of different frequency are called by different names since they have different sources and effects on matter.  In order of increasing frequency and decreasing wavelength these are: radio waves, microwaves, infrared radiation, <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible light</a>, ultraviolet radiation, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Electromagnetic waves are emitted by electrically <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">charged particles</a> undergoing acceleration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cloude_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cloude-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bettini_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bettini-5">[5]</a></sup> and these waves can subsequently interact with other charged particles, exerting force on them.  EM waves carry <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> away from their source particle and can impart those quantities to <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> with which they interact.  Electromagnetic radiation is associated with those EM waves that are free to propagate themselves ("radiate") without the continuing influence of the moving charges that produced them, because they have achieved sufficient distance from those charges. Thus, EMR is sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">far field</a>. In this language, the <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">near field</a> refers to EM fields near the charges and current that directly produced them specifically, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">electromagnetic induction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_induction" title="Electrostatic induction">electrostatic induction</a> phenomena.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, an alternate way of viewing EMR is that it consists of <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>, uncharged <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a> with zero <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> which are the <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quanta</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a>, responsible for all electromagnetic interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">Quantum electrodynamics</a> is the theory of how EMR interacts with matter on an atomic level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>  Quantum effects provide additional sources of EMR, such as the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_electron_transition" title="Atomic electron transition">transition of electrons</a> to lower <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy levels</a> in an atom and <a href="/wiki/Black-body_radiation" title="Black-body radiation">black-body radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tipler_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tipler-8">[8]</a></sup> The energy of an individual photon is <a href="/wiki/Quantization_(physics)" title="Quantization (physics)">quantized</a> and is greater for photons of higher frequency. This relationship is given by <a href="/wiki/Planck%E2%80%93Einstein_relation" title="Planck&#8211;Einstein relation">Planck\'s equation</a> <span class="nowrap"><i>E</i> = <i>h&#957;</i></span>, where <i>E</i> is the energy per photon, <i>&#957;</i> is the frequency of the photon, and <i>h</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck\'s constant">Planck\'s constant</a>. A single gamma ray photon, for example, might carry ~100,000 times the energy of a single photon of visible light.\n</p><p>The effects of EMR upon chemical compounds and biological organisms depend both upon the radiation\'s <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">power</a> and its frequency. EMR of visible or lower frequencies (i.e., visible light, infrared, microwaves, and radio waves) is called <i><a href="/wiki/Non-ionizing_radiation" title="Non-ionizing radiation">non-ionizing radiation</a></i>, because its photons do not individually have enough energy to <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionize</a> atoms or molecules or break <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a>. The effects of these radiations on chemical systems and living tissue are caused primarily by heating effects from the combined energy transfer of many photons. In contrast, high frequency ultraviolet, X-rays and gamma rays are called <i><a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a></i>, since individual photons of such high frequency have enough energy to <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionize</a> molecules or break <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a>. These radiations have the ability to cause <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a> and damage living cells beyond that resulting from simple heating, and can be a health hazard.\n</p>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/Classical_electromagnetism" title="Classical electromagnetism">Electrodynamics</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lorentz_force" title="Lorentz style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left">Scientists</div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory">Theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg/220px-VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg/330px-VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg/440px-VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VisibleEmrWavelengths.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shows the relative wavelengths of the electromagnetic waves of three different colours of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">light</a> (blue, green, and red) with a distance scale in micrometers along the x-axis.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell\'s equations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">Near and far field</a></div>\n<h4><span id="Maxwell.E2.80.99s_equations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Maxwell&#8217;s_equations">Maxwell&#8217;s equations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Maxwell&#8217;s equations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> derived a <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave_equation" title="Electromagnetic wave equation">wave form of the electric and magnetic equations</a>, thus uncovering the wave-like nature of electric and magnetic fields and their <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_(physics)" title="Symmetry (physics)">symmetry</a>. Because the speed of EM waves predicted by the wave equation coincided with the measured <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, Maxwell concluded that <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> itself is an EM wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Maxwell\'s equations were confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">Heinrich Hertz</a> through experiments with radio waves.\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell\'s equations</a>, a spatially varying <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> is always associated with a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> that changes over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Likewise, a spatially varying magnetic field is associated with specific changes over time in the electric field. In an electromagnetic wave, the changes in the electric field are always accompanied by a wave in the magnetic field in one direction, and vice versa. This relationship between the two occurs without either type of field causing the other; rather, they occur together in the same way that time and space changes occur together and are interlinked in <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>. In fact, magnetic fields can be viewed as electric fields in another frame of reference, and electric fields can be viewed as magnetic fields in another frame of reference, but they have equal significance as physics is the same in all frames of reference, so the close relationship between space and time changes here is more than an analogy. Together, these fields form a propagating electromagnetic wave, which moves out into space and need never again interact with the source. The distant EM field formed in this way by the acceleration of a charge carries energy with it that "radiates" away through space, hence the term.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Near_and_far_fields">Near and far fields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Near and far fields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">Near and far field</a> and <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A9nard%E2%80%93Wiechert_potential" title="Li&#233;nard&#8211;Wiechert potential">Li&#233;nard&#8211;Wiechert potential</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="329" data-file-width="618" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg/300px-FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg/450px-FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg/600px-FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FarNearFields-USP-4998112-1.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In electromagnetic radiation (such as microwaves from an antenna, shown here) the term applies only to the parts of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> that radiate into infinite space and decrease in intensity by an <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">inverse-square law</a> of power, so that the total radiation energy that crosses through an imaginary spherical surface is the same, no matter how far away from the antenna the spherical surface is drawn. Electromagnetic radiation thus includes the <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">far field</a> part of the electromagnetic field around a transmitter. A part of the "near-field" close to the transmitter, forms part of the changing <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a>, but does not count as electromagnetic radiation.</div></div></div>\n<p>Maxwell\'s equations established that some charges and currents ("sources") produce a local type of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">electromagnetic field</a> near them that does <i>not</i> have the behaviour of EMR. Currents directly produce a magnetic field, but it is of a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole" title="Magnetic dipole">magnetic dipole</a> type that dies out with distance from the current. In a similar manner, moving charges pushed apart in a conductor by a changing electrical potential (such as in an antenna) produce an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_dipole" title="Electric dipole">electric dipole</a> type electrical field, but this also declines with distance. These fields make up the <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">near-field</a> near the EMR source. Neither of these behaviours are responsible for EM radiation. Instead, they cause electromagnetic field behaviour that only efficiently transfers power to a receiver very close to the source, such as the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">magnetic induction</a> inside a <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformer</a>, or the feedback behaviour that happens close to the coil of a <a href="/wiki/Metal_detector" title="Metal detector">metal detector</a>. Typically, near-fields have a powerful effect on their own sources, causing an increased &#8220;load&#8221; (decreased <a href="/wiki/Electrical_reactance" title="Electrical reactance">electrical reactance</a>) in the source or transmitter, whenever energy is withdrawn from the EM field by a receiver. Otherwise, these fields do not &#8220;propagate&#8221; freely out into space, carrying their energy away without distance-limit, but rather oscillate, returning their energy to the transmitter if it is not received by a receiver.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>By contrast, the EM far-field is composed of <i>radiation</i> that is free of the transmitter in the sense that (unlike the case in an electrical transformer) the transmitter requires the same power to send these changes in the fields out, whether the signal is immediately picked up or not. This distant part of the electromagnetic field <i>is</i> "electromagnetic radiation" (also called the <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">far-field</a>). The far-fields propagate (radiate) without allowing the transmitter to affect them. This causes them to be independent in the sense that their existence and their energy, after they have left the transmitter, is completely independent of both transmitter and receiver. Due to <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a>, the amount of power passing through any spherical surface drawn around the source is the same. Because such a surface has an area proportional to the square of its distance from the source, the <a href="/wiki/Power_density" title="Power density">power density</a> of EM radiation always decreases with the inverse square of distance from the source; this is called the <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">inverse-square law</a>. This is in contrast to dipole parts of the EM field close to the source (the near-field), which varies in power according to an inverse cube power law, and thus does <i>not</i> transport a conserved amount of energy over distances, but instead fades with distance, with its energy (as noted) rapidly returning to the transmitter or absorbed by a nearby receiver (such as a transformer secondary coil).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The far-field (EMR) depends on a different mechanism for its production than the near-field, and upon different terms in Maxwell\'s equations. Whereas the magnetic part of the near-field is due to currents in the source, the magnetic field in EMR is due only to the local change in the electric field. In a similar way, while the electric field in the near-field is due directly to the charges and charge-separation in the source, the electric field in EMR is due to a change in the local magnetic field. Both processes for producing electric and magnetic EMR fields have a different dependence on distance than do near-field dipole electric and magnetic fields. That is why the EMR type of EM field becomes dominant in power &#8220;far&#8221; from sources. The term &#8220;far from sources&#8221; refers to how far from the source (moving at the speed of light) any portion of the outward-moving EM field is located, by the time that source currents are changed by the varying source potential, and the source has therefore begun to generate an outwardly moving EM field of a different phase.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>A more compact view of EMR is that the far-field that composes EMR is generally that part of the EM field that has traveled sufficient distance from the source, that it has become completely disconnected from any feedback to the charges and currents that were originally responsible for it. Now independent of the source charges, the EM field, as it moves farther away, is dependent only upon the accelerations of the charges that produced it. It no longer has a strong connection to the direct fields of the charges, or to the velocity of the charges (currents).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A9nard%E2%80%93Wiechert_potential" title="Li&#233;nard&#8211;Wiechert potential">Li&#233;nard&#8211;Wiechert potential</a> formulation of the electric and magnetic fields due to motion of a single particle (according to Maxwell\'s equations), the terms associated with acceleration of the particle are those that are responsible for the part of the field that is regarded as electromagnetic radiation. By contrast, the term associated with the changing static electric field of the particle and the magnetic term that results from the particle\'s uniform velocity, are both associated with the electromagnetic near-field, and do not comprise EM radiation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electromagneticwave3D.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="299" data-file-width="301" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Electromagneticwave3D.gif/220px-Electromagneticwave3D.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Electromagneticwave3D.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electromagneticwave3D.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Electromagnetic waves can be imagined as a self-propagating transverse oscillating wave of electric and magnetic fields. This 3D animation shows a plane linearly polarized wave propagating from left to right. Note that the electric and magnetic fields in such a wave are in-phase with each other, reaching minima and maxima together.</div></div></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrodynamics" title="Electrodynamics">Electrodynamics</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> of electromagnetic radiation, and <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a> is the physical phenomenon associated with the theory of electrodynamics. Electric and magnetic fields obey the properties of <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition</a>. Thus, a field due to any particular particle or time-varying electric or magnetic field contributes to the fields present in the same space due to other causes. Further, as they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(geometric)" title="Vector (geometric)">vector</a> fields, all magnetic and electric field vectors add together according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_addition" title="Vector addition">vector addition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> For example, in optics two or more coherent lightwaves may interact and by constructive or destructive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> yield a resultant irradiance deviating from the sum of the component irradiances of the individual lightwaves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Since light is an oscillation it is not affected by traveling through static electric or magnetic fields in a linear medium such as a vacuum. However, in nonlinear media, such as some <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystals</a>, interactions can occur between light and static electric and magnetic fields &#8212; these interactions include the <a href="/wiki/Faraday_effect" title="Faraday effect">Faraday effect</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kerr_effect" title="Kerr effect">Kerr effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crowther-1920_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-crowther-1920-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a>, a wave crossing from one medium to another of different <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> alters its <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">speed and direction</a> upon entering the new medium. The ratio of the refractive indices of the media determines the degree of refraction, and is summarized by <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell\'s law">Snell\'s law</a>. Light of composite wavelengths (natural sunlight) disperses into a visible <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">spectrum</a> passing through a prism, because of the wavelength-dependent <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Prism" title="Prism">prism</a> material (<a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">dispersion</a>); that is, each component wave within the composite light is bent a different amount.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>EM radiation exhibits both wave properties and <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">particle</a> properties at the same time (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave-particle_duality" title="Wave-particle duality">wave-particle duality</a>). Both wave and particle characteristics have been confirmed in many experiments. Wave characteristics are more apparent when EM radiation is measured over relatively large timescales and over large distances while particle characteristics are more evident when measuring small timescales and distances. For example, when electromagnetic radiation is absorbed by matter, particle-like properties will be more obvious when the average number of photons in the cube of the relevant wavelength is much smaller than 1. It is not too difficult to experimentally observe non-uniform deposition of energy when light is absorbed, however this alone is not evidence of "particulate" behavior. Rather, it reflects the quantum nature of <i>matter</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Demonstrating that the light itself is quantized, not merely its interaction with matter, is a more subtle affair.\n</p><p>Some experiments display both the wave and particle natures of electromagnetic waves, such as the self-interference of a single <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> When a single photon is sent through an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interferometer" title="Interferometer">interferometer</a>, it passes through both paths, interfering with itself, as waves do, yet is detected by a <a href="/wiki/Photomultiplier" title="Photomultiplier">photomultiplier</a> or other sensitive detector only once.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum theory</a> of the interaction between electromagnetic radiation and matter such as electrons is described by the theory of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">quantum electrodynamics</a>.\n</p><p>Electromagnetic waves can be <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarized</a>, reflected, refracted, <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffracted</a> or interfere with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_model">Wave model</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Wave model">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Right.Handed.Animation.305x190.255Colors.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="190" data-file-width="305" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Right.Handed.Animation.305x190.255Colors.gif/220px-Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Right.Handed.Animation.305x190.255Colors.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/81/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Right.Handed.Animation.305x190.255Colors.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Right.Handed.Animation.305x190.255Colors.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Representation of the electric field vector of a wave of circularly polarized electromagnetic radiation.</div></div></div><p> In homogeneous, isotropic media, electromagnetic radiation is a <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse wave</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> meaning that its oscillations are perpendicular to the direction of energy transfer and travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The electric and magnetic parts of the field stand in a fixed ratio of strengths in order to satisfy the two <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell equations</a> that specify how one is produced from the other. In dissipation less (lossless) media, these <b>E</b> and <b>B</b> fields are also in phase, with both reaching maxima and minima at the same points in space (see illustrations). A common misconception is that the <b>E</b> and <b>B</b> fields in electromagnetic radiation are out of phase because a change in one produces the other, and this would produce a phase difference between them as sinusoidal functions (as indeed happens in <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">electromagnetic induction</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">near-field</a> close to antennas). However, in the far-field EM radiation which is described by the two source-free Maxwell <a href="/wiki/Curl_(mathematics)" title="Curl (mathematics)">curl operator</a> equations, a more correct description is that a time-change in one type of field is proportional to a space-change in the other. These derivatives require that the <b>E</b> and <b>B</b> fields in EMR are in-phase (see math section below).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>An important aspect of light\'s nature is its frequency. The frequency of a wave is its rate of oscillation and is measured in <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">SI</a> unit of frequency, where one hertz is equal to one oscillation per <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a>. Light usually has multiple frequencies that sum to form the resultant wave. Different frequencies undergo different angles of refraction, a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_relation" title="Dispersion relation">dispersion</a>.\n</p><p>A wave consists of successive troughs and crests, and the distance between two adjacent crests or troughs is called the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a>. Waves of the electromagnetic spectrum vary in size, from very long radio waves the size of buildings to very short gamma rays smaller than atom nuclei. Frequency is inversely proportional to wavelength, according to the equation:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\displaystyle v=f\\lambda }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\displaystyle v=f\\lambda }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\displaystyle v=f\\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edd7e0d5f184b421f9c4b5b69861b807c8029e4f" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.86ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>v</i> is the speed of the wave (<i><a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">c</a></i> in a vacuum, or less in other media), <i>f</i> is the frequency and &#955; is the wavelength. As waves cross boundaries between different media, their speeds change but their frequencies remain constant.\n</p><p>Electromagnetic waves in free space must be solutions of Maxwell\'s <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave_equation" title="Electromagnetic wave equation">electromagnetic wave equation</a>. Two main classes of solutions are known, namely plane waves and spherical waves. The plane waves may be viewed as the limiting case of spherical waves at a very large (ideally infinite) distance from the source.  Both types of waves can have a waveform which is an arbitrary time function (so long as it is sufficiently differentiable to conform to the wave equation).  As with any time function, this can be decomposed by means of <a href="/wiki/Fourier_analysis" title="Fourier analysis">Fourier analysis</a> into its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequency_spectrum" title="Frequency spectrum">frequency spectrum</a>, or individual sinusoidal components, each of which contains a single frequency, amplitude and phase. Such a component wave is said to be <i>monochromatic</i>.  A monochromatic electromagnetic wave can be characterized by its frequency or wavelength, its peak amplitude, its phase relative to some reference phase, its direction of propagation and its polarization.\n</p><p>Interference is the superposition of two or more waves resulting in a new wave pattern. If the fields have components in the same direction, they constructively interfere, while opposite directions cause destructive interference. An example of interference caused by EMR is <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interference" title="Electromagnetic interference">electromagnetic interference</a> (EMI) or as it is more commonly known as, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_frequency_interference" title="Radio frequency interference">radio-frequency interference</a> (RFI).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  Additionally, multiple polarization signals can be combined (i.e. interfered) to form new states of polarization, which is known as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Parallel_polarization_state_generation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Parallel polarization state generation (page does not exist)">parallel polarization state generation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The energy in electromagnetic waves is sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiant energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Particle_model_and_quantum_theory">Particle model and quantum theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Particle model and quantum theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Quantization_(physics)" title="Quantization (physics)">Quantization (physics)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_optics" title="Quantum optics">Quantum optics</a></div>\n<p>An anomaly arose in the late 19th century involving a contradiction between the wave theory of light and measurements of the electromagnetic spectra that were being emitted by thermal radiators known as <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black bodies</a>. Physicists struggled with this problem, which later became known as the <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_catastrophe" title="Ultraviolet catastrophe">ultraviolet catastrophe</a>, unsuccessfully for many years. In 1900, <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Max Planck</a> developed a new theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law_of_black-body_radiation" title="Planck\'s law of black-body radiation">black-body radiation</a> that explained the observed spectrum. Planck\'s theory was based on the idea that black bodies emit light (and other electromagnetic radiation) only as discrete bundles or packets of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. These packets were called <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quanta</a>. Later, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> proposed that light quanta be regarded as real particles. Later the particle of light was given the name <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a>, to correspond with other particles being described around this time, such as the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>. A photon has an energy, <i>E</i>, proportional to its frequency, <i>f</i>, by\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E=hf={\\frac {hc}{\\lambda }}\\,\\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>\n<mspace width="negativethinmathspace"></mspace>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E=hf={\\frac {hc}{\\lambda }}\\,\\!}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E=hf={\\frac {hc}{\\lambda }}\\,\\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1f9c6febdb262c24c1541e6bc246b43853524d23" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; margin-right: -0.387ex; width:14.159ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>where <i>h</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck\'s constant">Planck\'s constant</a>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\lambda }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\lambda }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.355ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the wavelength and <i>c</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. This is sometimes known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%E2%80%93Einstein_equation" title="Planck&#8211;Einstein equation">Planck&#8211;Einstein equation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In quantum theory (see <a href="/wiki/First_quantization" title="First quantization">first quantization</a>) the energy of the photons is thus directly proportional to the frequency of the EMR wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Likewise, the momentum <i>p</i> of a photon is also proportional to its frequency and inversely proportional to its wavelength:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle p={E \\over c}={hf \\over c}={h \\over \\lambda }.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>p</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>f</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mi>h</mi>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle p={E \\over c}={hf \\over c}={h \\over \\lambda }.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="p={E \\over c}={hf \\over c}={h \\over \\lambda }." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/66e5511112840371e4b53e317aa80144f33af0dd" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:19.458ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The source of Einstein\'s proposal that light was composed of particles (or could act as particles in some circumstances) was an experimental anomaly not explained by the wave theory: the <a href="/wiki/Photoelectric_effect" title="Photoelectric effect">photoelectric effect</a>, in which light striking a metal surface ejected electrons from the surface, causing an <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> to flow across an applied <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a>. Experimental measurements demonstrated that the energy of individual ejected electrons was proportional to the <i><a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a></i>, rather than the <i><a href="/wiki/Intensity_(physics)" title="Intensity (physics)">intensity</a></i>, of the light. Furthermore, below a certain minimum frequency, which depended on the particular metal, no current would flow regardless of the intensity. These observations appeared to contradict the wave theory, and for years physicists tried in vain to find an explanation. In 1905, Einstein explained this puzzle by resurrecting the particle theory of light to explain the observed effect. Because of the preponderance of evidence in favor of the wave theory, however, Einstein\'s ideas were met initially with great skepticism among established physicists. Eventually Einstein\'s explanation was accepted as new particle-like behavior of light was observed, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compton_effect" title="Compton effect">Compton effect</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>As a photon is absorbed by an <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a>, it <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excites</a> the atom, elevating an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> to a higher <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy level</a> (one that is on average farther from the nucleus). When an electron in an excited molecule or atom descends to a lower energy level, it emits a photon of light at a frequency corresponding to the energy difference. Since the energy levels of electrons in atoms are discrete, each element and each molecule emits and absorbs its own characteristic frequencies. Immediate photon emission is called <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a>, a type of <a href="/wiki/Photoluminescence" title="Photoluminescence">photoluminescence</a>. An example is visible light emitted from fluorescent paints, in response to ultraviolet (<a href="/wiki/Blacklight" title="Blacklight">blacklight</a>). Many other fluorescent emissions are known in spectral bands other than visible light. Delayed emission is called <a href="/wiki/Phosphorescence" title="Phosphorescence">phosphorescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Wave.E2.80.93particle_duality"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave&#8211;particle_duality">Wave&#8211;particle duality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Wave&#8211;particle duality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave&#8211;particle duality">Wave&#8211;particle duality</a></div>\n<p>The modern theory that explains the nature of light includes the notion of wave&#8211;particle duality. More generally, the theory states that everything has both a particle nature and a wave nature, and various experiments can be done to bring out one or the other. The particle nature is more easily discerned using an object with a large mass. A bold proposition by <a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Broglie" title="Louis de Broglie">Louis de Broglie</a> in 1924 led the scientific community to realize that <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> also exhibited wave&#8211;particle duality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_and_particle_effects_of_electromagnetic_radiation">Wave and particle effects of electromagnetic radiation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wave and particle effects of electromagnetic radiation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Together, wave and particle effects fully explain the emission and absorption spectra of EM radiation. The matter-composition of the medium through which the light travels determines the nature of the absorption and emission spectrum. These bands correspond to the allowed energy levels in the atoms. Dark bands in the <a href="/wiki/Absorption_spectroscopy" title="Absorption spectroscopy">absorption spectrum</a> are due to the atoms in an intervening medium between source and observer. The atoms absorb certain frequencies of the light between emitter and detector/eye, then emit them in all directions. A dark band appears to the detector, due to the radiation scattered out of the beam. For instance, dark bands in the light emitted by a distant <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> are due to the atoms in the star\'s atmosphere. A similar phenomenon occurs for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Emission (electromagnetic radiation)">emission</a>, which is seen when an emitting gas glows due to excitation of the atoms from any mechanism, including heat. As electrons descend to lower energy levels, a spectrum is emitted that represents the jumps between the energy levels of the electrons, but lines are seen because again emission happens only at particular energies after excitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> An example is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Emission (electromagnetic radiation)">emission</a> spectrum of <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Rapidly moving electrons are most sharply accelerated when they encounter a region of force, so they are responsible for producing much of the highest frequency electromagnetic radiation observed in nature.\n</p><p>These phenomena can aid various chemical determinations for the composition of gases lit from behind (absorption spectra) and for glowing gases (emission spectra). Spectroscopy (for example) determines what <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> comprise a particular star. Spectroscopy is also used in the determination of the distance of a star, using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_shift" title="Red shift">red shift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propagation_speed">Propagation speed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Propagation speed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">Speed of light</a></div>\n<p>When any wire (or other conducting object such as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_(electronics)" title="Antenna (electronics)">antenna</a>) conducts <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">alternating current</a>, electromagnetic radiation is propagated at the same frequency as the current. In many such situations it is possible to identify an electrical dipole moment that arises from separation of charges due to the exciting electrical potential, and this dipole moment oscillates in time, as the charges move back and forth. This oscillation at a given frequency gives rise to changing electric and magnetic fields, which then set the electromagnetic radiation in motion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>At the quantum level, electromagnetic radiation is produced when the wavepacket of a charged particle oscillates or otherwise accelerates. Charged particles in a <a href="/wiki/Stationary_state" title="Stationary state">stationary state</a> do not move, but a superposition of such states may result in a transition state that has an <a href="/wiki/Electric_dipole_moment" title="Electric dipole moment">electric dipole moment</a> that oscillates in time. This oscillating dipole moment is responsible for the phenomenon of radiative transition between quantum states of a charged particle. Such states occur (for example) in atoms when photons are radiated as the atom shifts from one stationary state to another.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>As a wave, light is characterized by a velocity (the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>), <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>. As particles, light is a stream of <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons.</a> Each has an energy related to the frequency of the wave given by <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck" title="Max Planck">Planck\'s</a> relation <i>E = hf</i>, where <i>E</i> is the energy of the photon, <i>h</i> = 6.626 &#215; 10<sup>&#8722;34</sup> J&#183;s is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck\'s constant">Planck\'s constant</a>, and <i>f</i> is the frequency of the wave.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>One rule is obeyed regardless of circumstances: EM radiation in a vacuum travels at the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>, <i>relative to the observer</i>, regardless of the observer\'s velocity. (This observation led to Einstein\'s development of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a>.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In a medium (other than vacuum), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velocity_of_propagation" title="Velocity of propagation">velocity factor</a> or <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> are considered, depending on frequency and application. Both of these are ratios of the speed in a medium to speed in a vacuum.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_theory_of_relativity">Special theory of relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Special theory of relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_theory_of_relativity" title="Special theory of relativity">Special theory of relativity</a></div>\n<p>By the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_nineteenth_century" title="Late nineteenth century">late nineteenth century</a>, various experimental anomalies could not be explained by the simple wave theory. One of these anomalies involved a controversy over the speed of light. The speed of light and other EMR predicted by Maxwell\'s equations did not appear unless the equations were modified in a way first suggested by <a href="/wiki/George_Francis_FitzGerald" title="George Francis FitzGerald">FitzGerald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Lorentz</a> (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_special_relativity" title="History of special relativity">history of special relativity</a>), or else otherwise that speed would depend on the speed of observer relative to the "medium" (called <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">luminiferous aether</a>) which supposedly "carried" the electromagnetic wave (in a manner analogous to the way air carries sound waves). Experiments failed to find any observer effect. In 1905, Einstein proposed that space and time appeared to be velocity-changeable entities for light propagation and all other processes and laws. These changes accounted for the constancy of the speed of light and all electromagnetic radiation, from the viewpoints of all observers&#8212;even those in relative motion.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_discovery">History of discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History of discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_electromagnetic_theory" title="History of electromagnetic theory">History of electromagnetic theory</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_electromagnetic_theory" title="Timeline of electromagnetic theory">Timeline of electromagnetic theory</a></div>\n<p>Electromagnetic radiation of wavelengths other than those of visible light were discovered in the early 19th century. The discovery of <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation is ascribed to astronomer <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a>, who published his results in 1800 before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_London" title="Royal Society of London">Royal Society of London</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerschelRSIR_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-HerschelRSIR-33">[33]</a></sup> Herschel used a glass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triangular_prism_(optics)" title="Triangular prism (optics)">prism</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refract" title="Refract">refract</a> light from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and detected invisible rays that caused heating beyond the <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a> part of the spectrum, through an increase in the temperature recorded with a <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometer</a>. These "calorific rays" were later termed infrared.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In 1801, German physicist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wilhelm_Ritter" title="Johann Wilhelm Ritter">Johann Wilhelm Ritter</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> in an experiment similar to Hershel\'s, using sunlight and a glass prism. Ritter noted that invisible rays near the violet edge of a solar spectrum dispersed by a triangular prism darkened <a href="/wiki/Silver_chloride" title="Silver chloride">silver chloride</a> preparations more quickly than did the nearby violet light. Ritter\'s experiments were an early precursor to what would become photography. Ritter noted that the ultraviolet rays (which at first were called "chemical rays") were capable of causing chemical reactions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="837" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg/170px-James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg/255px-James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg/340px-James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Clerk_Maxwell_sitting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1862-4 <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> developed equations for the electromagnetic field which suggested that waves in the field would travel with a speed that was very close to the known speed of light. Maxwell therefore suggested that visible light (as well as invisible infrared and ultraviolet rays by inference) all consisted of propagating disturbances (or radiation) in the electromagnetic field. Radio waves were first produced deliberately by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Hertz" title="Heinrich Hertz">Heinrich Hertz</a> in 1887, using electrical circuits calculated to produce oscillations at a much lower frequency than that of visible light, following recipes for producing oscillating charges and currents suggested by Maxwell\'s equations. Hertz also developed ways to detect these waves, and produced and characterized what were later termed <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwaves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeans_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeans-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>286,7</span></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_R%C3%B6ntgen" title="Wilhelm R&#246;ntgen">Wilhelm R&#246;ntgen</a> discovered and named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">X-rays</a>. After experimenting with high voltages applied to an evacuated tube on 8 November 1895, he noticed a fluorescence on a nearby plate of coated glass. In one month, he discovered X-rays\' main properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeans_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeans-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>307</span></sup>\n</p><p>The last portion of the EM spectrum to be discovered was associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Henri Becquerel</a> found that <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> salts caused fogging of an unexposed photographic plate through a covering paper in a manner similar to X-rays, and <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a> discovered that only certain elements gave off these rays of energy, soon discovering the intense radiation of <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>. The radiation from pitchblende was differentiated into alpha rays (<a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>) and beta rays (<a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particles</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> through simple experimentation in 1899, but these proved to be charged particulate types of radiation. However, in 1900 the French scientist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Villard" title="Paul Villard">Paul Villard</a> discovered a third neutrally charged and especially penetrating type of radiation from radium, and after he described it, Rutherford realized it must be yet a third type of radiation, which in 1903 Rutherford named <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>. In 1910 British physicist <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Bragg" title="William Henry Bragg">William Henry Bragg</a> demonstrated that gamma rays are electromagnetic radiation, not particles, and in 1914 Rutherford and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Andrade" title="Edward Andrade">Edward Andrade</a> measured their wavelengths, finding that they were similar to X-rays but with shorter wavelengths and higher frequency, although a \'cross-over\' between X and gamma rays makes it possible to have X-rays with a higher energy (and hence shorter wavelength) than gamma rays and vice versa. The origin of the ray differentiates them, gamma rays tend to be a natural phenomena originating from the unstable nucleus of an atom and X-rays are electrically generated (and hence man-made) unless they are as a result of <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">bremsstrahlung</a> X-radiation caused by the interaction of fast moving particles (such as beta particles) colliding with certain materials, usually of higher atomic numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeans_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jeans-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>308,9</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_spectrum">Electromagnetic spectrum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Electromagnetic spectrum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EM_spectrumrevised.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1070" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/EM_spectrumrevised.png/500px-EM_spectrumrevised.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/EM_spectrumrevised.png/750px-EM_spectrumrevised.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/EM_spectrumrevised.png/1000px-EM_spectrumrevised.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EM_spectrumrevised.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">Electromagnetic spectrum</a> with visible light highlighted</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">Electromagnetic spectrum</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:326px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_spectrum.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="324" decoding="async" height="447" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Light_spectrum.svg/324px-Light_spectrum.svg.png" width="324"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><b>Legend:</b><br/> &#947; = <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">Gamma rays</a><br/> <br/> HX = Hard <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a><br/> SX = Soft X-Rays<br/> <br/> EUV = Extreme-<a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a><br/> NUV = Near-ultraviolet<br/> <br/> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">Visible light</a> (colored bands)<br/> <br/> NIR = Near-<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a><br/> MIR = Mid-infrared<br/> FIR = Far-infrared<br/> <br/> EHF = <a href="/wiki/Extremely_high_frequency" title="Extremely high frequency">Extremely high frequency</a> (microwaves)<br/> SHF = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-high_frequency" title="Super-high frequency">Super-high frequency</a> (microwaves)<br/> <br/> UHF = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultrahigh_frequency" title="Ultrahigh frequency">Ultrahigh frequency</a> (radio waves)<br/> VHF = <a href="/wiki/Very_high_frequency" title="Very high frequency">Very high frequency</a> (radio)<br/> HF = <a href="/wiki/High_frequency" title="High frequency">High frequency</a> (radio)<br/> MF = <a href="/wiki/Medium_frequency" title="Medium frequency">Medium frequency</a> (radio)<br/> LF = <a href="/wiki/Low_frequency" title="Low frequency">Low frequency</a> (radio)<br/> VLF = <a href="/wiki/Very_low_frequency" title="Very low frequency">Very low frequency</a> (radio)<br/> VF = <a href="/wiki/Voice_frequency" title="Voice frequency">Voice frequency</a><br/> ULF = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultra-low_frequency" title="Ultra-low frequency">Ultra-low frequency</a> (radio)<br/> SLF = <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-low_frequency" title="Super-low frequency">Super-low frequency</a> (radio)<br/> ELF = <a href="/wiki/Extremely_low_frequency" title="Extremely low frequency">Extremely low frequency</a>(radio)</div></div></div>\n<p>EM radiation (the designation \'radiation\' excludes static electric and magnetic and <a href="/wiki/Near_and_far_field" title="Near and far field">near fields</a>) is classified by wavelength into <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma rays</a>. Arbitrary electromagnetic waves can be expressed by <a href="/wiki/Fourier_analysis" title="Fourier analysis">Fourier analysis</a> in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinusoidal" title="Sinusoidal">sinusoidal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monochromatic" title="Monochromatic">monochromatic</a> waves, which in turn can each be classified into these regions of the EMR spectrum.\n</p><p>For certain classes of EM waves, the waveform is most usefully treated as <i>random</i>, and then spectral analysis must be done by slightly different mathematical techniques appropriate to random or <a href="/wiki/Stochastic_process" title="Stochastic process">stochastic processes</a>. In such cases, the individual frequency components are represented in terms of their <i>power</i> content, and the phase information is not preserved. Such a representation is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_spectral_density" title="Power spectral density">power spectral density</a> of the random process. Random electromagnetic radiation requiring this kind of analysis is, for example, encountered in the interior of stars, and in certain other very wideband forms of radiation such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero_point_field" title="Zero point field">Zero point wave field</a> of the electromagnetic vacuum.\n</p><p>The behavior of EM radiation depends on its frequency. Lower frequencies have longer wavelengths, and higher frequencies have shorter wavelengths, and are associated with photons of higher energy. There is no fundamental limit known to these wavelengths or energies, at either end of the spectrum, although photons with energies near the <a href="/wiki/Planck_energy" title="Planck energy">Planck energy</a> or exceeding it (far too high to have ever been observed) will require new physical theories to describe.\n</p><p>Soundwaves are not electromagnetic radiation<b>.</b> At the lower end of the electromagnetic spectrum, about 20&#160;Hz to about 20&#160;kHz, are frequencies that might be considered in the audio range. However, electromagnetic waves cannot be directly perceived by human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ears" title="Ears">ears</a>. Sound waves are instead the oscillating compression of molecules. To be heard, electromagnetic radiation must be converted to pressure waves of the fluid in which the <a href="/wiki/Ear" title="Ear">ear</a> is located (whether the fluid is air, water or something else).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions_as_a_function_of_frequency">Interactions as a function of frequency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Interactions as a function of frequency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When EM radiation interacts with matter, its behavior changes qualitatively as its frequency changes.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_and_microwave">Radio and microwave</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Radio and microwave">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>At radio and microwave frequencies, EMR interacts with matter largely as a bulk collection of charges which are spread out over large numbers of affected atoms. In <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductors</a>, such induced bulk movement of charges (<a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric currents</a>) results in absorption of the EMR, or else separations of charges that cause generation of new EMR (effective reflection of the EMR). An example is absorption or emission of radio waves by antennas, or absorption of microwaves by water or other molecules with an electric dipole moment, as for example inside a <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">microwave oven</a>. These interactions produce either electric currents or heat, or both.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrared">Infrared</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Infrared">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Like radio and microwave, infrared also is reflected by metals (as is most EMR into the ultraviolet).
