However, unlike lower-frequency radio and microwave radiation, Infrared EMR commonly interacts with dipoles present in single molecules, which change as atoms vibrate at the ends of a single chemical bond. It is consequently absorbed by a wide range of substances, causing them to increase in temperature as the vibrations dissipate as <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a>. The same process, run in reverse, causes bulk substances to radiate in the infrared spontaneously (see <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a> section below).\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Visible_light">Visible light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Visible light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>As frequency increases into the visible range, photons have enough energy to change the bond structure of some individual molecules. It is not a coincidence that this happens in the "visible range," as the <a href="/wiki/Visual_system" title="Visual system">mechanism of vision</a> involves the change in bonding of a single molecule (<a href="/wiki/Retinal" title="Retinal">retinal</a>) which absorbs light in the <a href="/wiki/Rhodopsin" title="Rhodopsin">rhodopsin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> of the human eye. <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">Photosynthesis</a> becomes possible in this range as well, for similar reasons, as a single molecule of <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> is excited by a single photon. Animals that detect infrared make use of small packets of water that change temperature, in an essentially thermal process that involves many photons (see <a href="/wiki/Infrared_sensing_in_snakes" title="Infrared sensing in snakes">infrared sensing in snakes</a>). For this reason, infrared, microwaves and radio waves are thought to damage molecules and biological tissue only by bulk heating, not excitation from single photons of the radiation.\n</p><p>Visible light is able to affect a few molecules with single photons, but usually not in a permanent or damaging way, in the absence of power high enough to increase temperature to damaging levels. However, in plant tissues that conduct photosynthesis, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carotenoids" title="Carotenoids">carotenoids</a> act to quench electronically excited chlorophyll produced by visible light in a process called <a href="/wiki/Non-photochemical_quenching" title="Non-photochemical quenching">non-photochemical quenching</a>, in order to prevent reactions that would otherwise interfere with photosynthesis at high light levels. Limited evidence indicate that some <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> are created by visible light in skin, and that these may have some role in photoaging, in the same manner as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_A" title="Ultraviolet A">ultraviolet A</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-r1-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ultraviolet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>As frequency increases into the ultraviolet, photons now carry enough energy (about three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">electron volts</a> or more) to excite certain doubly bonded molecules into permanent chemical rearrangement. In <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, this causes lasting damage. DNA is also indirectly damaged by reactive oxygen species produced by ultraviolet A (UVA), which has energy too low to damage DNA directly. This is why ultraviolet at all wavelengths can damage DNA, and is capable of causing cancer, and (for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UVB" title="UVB">UVB</a>) skin burns (sunburn) that are far worse than would be produced by simple heating (temperature increase) effects. This property of causing molecular damage that is out of proportion to heating effects, is characteristic of all EMR with frequencies at the visible light range and above. These properties of high-frequency EMR are due to quantum effects that permanently damage materials and tissues at the molecular level.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>At the higher end of the ultraviolet range, the energy of photons becomes large enough to impart enough energy to electrons to cause them to be liberated from the atom, in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoionisation" title="Photoionisation">photoionisation</a>. The energy required for this is always larger than about 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">electron volts</a> (eV) corresponding with wavelengths smaller than 124&#160;nm (some sources suggest a more realistic cutoff of 33 eV, which is the energy required to ionize water). This high end of the ultraviolet spectrum with energies in the approximate ionization range, is sometimes called "extreme UV." Ionizing UV is strongly filtered by the Earth\'s atmosphere).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="X-rays_and_gamma_rays">X-rays and gamma rays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: X-rays and gamma rays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Electromagnetic radiation composed of photons that carry minimum-ionization energy, or more, (which includes the entire spectrum with shorter wavelengths), is therefore termed <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>. (Many other kinds of ionizing radiation are made of non-EM particles). Electromagnetic-type ionizing radiation extends from the extreme ultraviolet to all higher frequencies and shorter wavelengths, which means that all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">X-rays</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma rays</a> qualify. These are capable of the most severe types of molecular damage, which can happen in biology to any type of biomolecule, including mutation and cancer, and often at great depths below the skin, since the higher end of the X-ray spectrum, and all of the gamma ray spectrum, penetrate matter.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere_and_magnetosphere">Atmosphere and magnetosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Atmosphere and magnetosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shortwave_radio" title="Shortwave radio">shortwave radio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skywave" title="Skywave">skywave</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="780" data-file-width="1650" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg/500px-Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg/750px-Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg/1000px-Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atmospheric_electromagnetic_opacity.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rough plot of Earth\'s atmospheric absorption and scattering (or <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opacity</a>) of various <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of electromagnetic radiation</div></div></div>\n<p>Most UV and X-rays are blocked by absorption first from molecular <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and then (for wavelengths in the upper UV) from the electronic excitation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioxygen" title="Dioxygen">dioxygen</a> and finally <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> at the mid-range of UV. Only 30% of the Sun\'s ultraviolet light reaches the ground, and almost all of this is well transmitted.\n</p><p>Visible light is well transmitted in air, as it is not energetic enough to excite nitrogen, oxygen, or ozone, but too energetic to excite molecular vibrational frequencies of water vapor.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Absorption bands in the infrared are due to modes of vibrational excitation in water vapor. However, at energies too low to excite water vapor, the atmosphere becomes transparent again, allowing free transmission of most microwave and radio waves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Finally, at radio wavelengths longer than 10 meters or so (about 30&#160;MHz), the air in the lower atmosphere remains transparent to radio, but plasma in certain layers of the <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> begins to interact with radio waves (see <a href="/wiki/Skywave" title="Skywave">skywave</a>). This property allows some longer wavelengths (100 meters or 3&#160;MHz) to be reflected and results in <a href="/wiki/Shortwave_radio" title="Shortwave radio">shortwave radio</a> beyond line-of-sight. However, <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere#D_layer" title="Ionosphere">certain ionospheric effects</a> begin to block incoming radiowaves from space, when their frequency is less than about 10&#160;MHz (wavelength longer than about 30 meters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Types_and_sources.2C_classed_by_spectral_band"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_and_sources,_classed_by_spectral_band">Types and sources, classed by spectral band</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Types and sources, classed by spectral band">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">Electromagnetic spectrum</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_waves">Radio waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Radio waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">Radio waves</a></div>\n<p>Radio waves have the least amount of energy and the lowest frequency. When radio waves impinge upon a <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">conductor</a>, they couple to the conductor, travel along it and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_frequency_induction" title="Radio frequency induction">induce</a> an electric current on the conductor surface by moving the electrons of the conducting material in correlated bunches of charge. Such effects can cover macroscopic distances in conductors (such as radio antennas), since the wavelength of radiowaves is long.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microwaves">Microwaves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Microwaves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwaves" title="Microwaves">Microwaves</a></div>\n<p>Microwaves are a form of electromagnetic radiation with wavelengths ranging from as long as one meter to as short as one millimeter; with frequencies between 300&#160;MHz (0.3&#160;GHz) and 300&#160;GHz.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrared_2">Infrared</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Infrared">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">Infrared</a></div>\n<table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2017</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visible_light_2">Visible light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Visible light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">Light</a></div>\n<p>Natural sources produce EM radiation across the spectrum. EM radiation with a <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> between approximately 400 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a> and 700&#160;nm is directly detected by the <a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">human eye</a> and perceived as visible <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a>. Other wavelengths, especially nearby infrared (longer than 700&#160;nm) and ultraviolet (shorter than 400&#160;nm) are also sometimes referred to as light.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultraviolet_2">Ultraviolet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Ultraviolet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a></div>\n<table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2017</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="X-rays">X-rays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: X-rays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">X-rays</a></div>\n<table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2017</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gamma_rays">Gamma rays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Gamma rays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">Gamma rays</a></div>\n<table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section is empty.</b> <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2017</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_and_electromagnetic_radiation_as_a_form_of_heat">Thermal and electromagnetic radiation as a form of heat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Thermal and electromagnetic radiation as a form of heat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">Thermal radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law" title="Planck\'s law">Planck\'s law</a></div>\n<p>The basic structure of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> involves charged particles bound together. When electromagnetic radiation impinges on matter, it causes the charged particles to oscillate and gain energy. The ultimate fate of this energy depends on the context. It could be immediately re-radiated and appear as scattered, reflected, or transmitted radiation. It may get dissipated into other microscopic motions within the matter, coming to <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a> and manifesting itself as <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a>, or even <a href="/wiki/Temperature#Kinetic_theory_approach_to_temperature" title="Temperature">kinetic energy</a>, in the material. With a few exceptions related to high-energy photons (such as <a href="/wiki/Fluorescence" title="Fluorescence">fluorescence</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harmonic_generation" title="Harmonic generation">harmonic generation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_reaction" title="Photochemical reaction">photochemical reactions</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">photovoltaic effect</a> for ionizing radiations at far ultraviolet, X-ray and gamma radiation), absorbed electromagnetic radiation simply deposits its energy by <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heating</a> the material. This happens for infrared, microwave and radio wave radiation. Intense radio waves can thermally burn living tissue and can cook food. In addition to infrared <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a>, sufficiently intense visible and ultraviolet lasers can easily set paper afire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Ionizing radiation creates high-speed electrons in a material and breaks chemical bonds, but after these electrons collide many times with other atoms eventually most of the energy becomes thermal energy all in a tiny fraction of a second. This process makes ionizing radiation far more dangerous per unit of energy than non-ionizing radiation. This caveat also applies to UV, even though almost all of it is not ionizing, because UV can damage molecules due to electronic excitation, which is far greater per unit energy than heating effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Infrared radiation in the spectral distribution of a <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> is usually considered a form of heat, since it has an equivalent temperature and is associated with an entropy change per unit of thermal energy. However, "heat" is a technical term in physics and thermodynamics and is often confused with thermal energy. Any type of electromagnetic energy can be transformed into thermal energy in interaction with matter. Thus, <i>any</i> electromagnetic radiation can "heat" (in the sense of increase the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> temperature of) a material, when it is absorbed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The inverse or time-reversed process of absorption is thermal radiation. Much of the thermal energy in matter consists of random motion of charged particles, and this energy can be radiated away from the matter. The resulting radiation may subsequently be absorbed by another piece of matter, with the deposited energy heating the material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The electromagnetic radiation in an opaque cavity at thermal equilibrium is effectively a form of thermal energy, having maximum <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">radiation entropy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_effects">Biological effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Biological effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation_and_health" title="Electromagnetic radiation and health">Electromagnetic radiation and health</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_radiation_and_health" title="Mobile phone radiation and health">Mobile phone radiation and health</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Bioelectromagnetics" title="Bioelectromagnetics">Bioelectromagnetics</a> is the study of the interactions and effects of EM radiation on living organisms. The effects of electromagnetic radiation upon living cells, including those in humans, depends upon the radiation\'s power and frequency. For low-frequency radiation (radio waves to visible light) the best-understood effects are those due to radiation power alone, acting through heating when radiation is absorbed. For these thermal effects, frequency is important only as it affects penetration into the organism (for example, microwaves penetrate better than infrared). It is widely accepted that low frequency fields that are too weak to cause significant heating could not possibly have any biological effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binhi_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Binhi-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite the commonly accepted results, some research has been conducted to show that weaker <i>non-thermal</i> electromagnetic fields, (including weak ELF magnetic fields, although the latter does not strictly qualify as EM radiation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binhi_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Binhi-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>), and modulated RF and microwave fields have biological effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aalto_S,_Haarala_C,_Br&#252;ck_A,_Sipil&#228;_H,_H&#228;m&#228;l&#228;inen_H,_Rinne_JO_2006_885&#8211;90_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aalto_S,_Haarala_C,_Br&#252;ck_A,_Sipil&#228;_H,_H&#228;m&#228;l&#228;inen_H,_Rinne_JO_2006_885&#8211;90-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Fundamental mechanisms of the interaction between biological material and electromagnetic fields at non-thermal levels are not fully understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Binhi_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Binhi-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> has classified radio frequency electromagnetic radiation as <a href="/wiki/List_of_IARC_Group_2B_carcinogens" title="List of IARC Group 2B carcinogens">Group 2B</a> - possibly carcinogenic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> This group contains possible carcinogens such as lead, DDT, and styrene. For example, epidemiological studies looking for a relationship between cell phone use and brain cancer development, have been largely inconclusive, save to demonstrate that the effect, if it exists, cannot be a large one.\n</p><p>At higher frequencies (visible and beyond), the effects of individual photons begin to become important, as these now have enough energy individually to directly or indirectly damage biological molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> All UV frequences have been classed as Group 1 carcinogens by the World Health Organization. Ultraviolet radiation from sun exposure is the primary cause of skin cancer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review05_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Review05-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thus, at UV frequencies and higher (and probably somewhat also in the visible range),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-r1-35">[35]</a></sup> electromagnetic radiation does more damage to biological systems than simple heating predicts. This is most obvious in the "far" (or "extreme") ultraviolet. UV, with X-ray and gamma radiation, are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a> due to the ability of photons of this radiation to produce <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radicals</a> in materials (including living tissue). Since such radiation can severely damage life at energy levels that produce little heating, it is considered far more dangerous (in terms of damage-produced per unit of energy, or power) than the rest of the electromagnetic spectrum.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_as_weapon">Use as weapon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Use as weapon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directed_energy_weapons#Microwave_weapons" title="Directed energy weapons">Directed energy weapons &#167;&#160;Microwave weapons</a></div>\n<p>The heat ray is an application of EMR that makes use of microwave frequencies to create an unpleasant heating effect in the upper layer of the skin.  A publicly known heat ray weapon called the <a href="/wiki/Active_Denial_System" title="Active Denial System">Active Denial System</a> was developed by the US military as an experimental weapon to deny the enemy access to an area.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  A <a href="/wiki/Death_ray" title="Death ray">death ray</a> is a weapon that delivers heat ray electromagnetic energy at levels that injure human tissue.  The inventor of the death ray, <a href="/wiki/Harry_Grindell_Matthews" title="Harry Grindell Matthews">Harry Grindell Matthews</a>,  claims to have lost sight in his left eye while developing his death ray weapon based on a primitive microwave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetron" title="Magnetron">magnetron</a> from the 1920s (note that a typical <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">microwave oven</a> induces a tissue damaging cooking effect inside the oven at about 2 kV/m).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivation_from_electromagnetic_theory">Derivation from electromagnetic theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electromagnetic_radiation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Derivation from electromagnetic theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave_equation" title="Electromagnetic wave equation">Electromagnetic wave equation</a></div>\n<p>Electromagnetic waves are predicted by the classical laws of electricity and magnetism, known as <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell\'s equations">Maxwell\'s equations</a>. There are nontrivial solutions of the homogeneous Maxwell\'s equations (without charges or currents), describing <i>waves</i> of changing electric and magnetic fields. Beginning with Maxwell\'s equations in <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">free space</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="nowrap" style="vertical-align:middle; width:1.757ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {B} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {B} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cafb0ef39b0f5ffa23c170aa7f7b4e718327c4d1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.901ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> are the <a href="/wiki/Vector_field" title="Vector field">vector fields</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_Field" title="Electric Field">Electric Field</a> (measured in <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">V</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter" title="Meter">m</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtons" title="Newtons">N</a>/<a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">C</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_Field" title="Magnetic Field">Magnetic Field</a> (measured in <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">T</a> or <a href="/wiki/Weber_(unit)" title="Weber (unit)">Wb</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Square_meter" title="Square meter">m<sup>2</sup></a>), respectively;</dd>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nabla \\cdot X}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8711;<!-- &#8711; --></mi>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mi>X</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nabla \\cdot X}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\nabla \\cdot X}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c74882cce4567c178bf47ae9ebf4d7488316112a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.595ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> yields the <a href="/wiki/Divergence" title="Divergence">divergence</a> field, and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\nabla \\times X}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8711;<!-- &#8711; --></mi>\n<mo>&#215;<!-- &#215; --></mo>\n<mi>X</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nabla \\times X}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\nabla \\times X}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a30bdbd8b3e6eccbda1979e7c27a2e67d8570a76" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>&#949;<!-- &#949; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle c_{0}={\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {\\mu _{0}\\varepsilon _{0}}}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle c_{0}={\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {\\mu _{0}\\varepsilon _{0}}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/202de6a29dada4774a8b1316e70987e376ca2a03" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:13.172ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl></dd></dl>\n<p>This is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in vacuum. Thus Maxwell\'s equations connect the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permittivity" title="Vacuum permittivity">vacuum permittivity</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\varepsilon _{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#949;<!-- &#949; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\varepsilon _{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\varepsilon _{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/acb0a8377db20e42274444cb181d51b5532b5844" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.138ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>, the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permeability" title="Vacuum permeability">vacuum permeability</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mu _{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mu _{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mu _{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe2fd9b8decb38a3cd158e7b6c0c6e2d987fefcc" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:2.456ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, and the speed of light, <i>c</i><sub>0</sub>, via the above equation. This relationship had been discovered by <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber" title="Wilhelm Eduard Weber">Wilhelm Eduard Weber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Kohlrausch" title="Rudolf Kohlrausch">Rudolf Kohlrausch</a> prior to the development of Maxwell\'s electrodynamics, however Maxwell was the first to produce a field theory consistent with waves traveling at the speed of light.\n</p><p>These are only two equations versus the original four, so more information pertains to these waves hidden within Maxwell\'s equations. A generic vector wave for the electric field.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} =\\mathbf {E} _{0}f\\left({\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\cdot \\mathbf {x} -c_{0}t\\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">k</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">^<!-- ^ --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; width:22.35ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Here, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} _{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} _{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {E} _{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75ee4610a644d1b4e038ff152ef47f067925f722" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>f</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">k</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">^<!-- ^ --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8901;<!-- &#8901; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">x</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\nabla \\times \\mathbf {E} ={\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\times \\mathbf {E} _{0}f\'\\left({\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\cdot \\mathbf {x} -c_{0}t\\right)=-{\\frac {\\partial \\mathbf {B} }{\\partial t}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\nabla \\times \\mathbf {E} ={\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\times \\mathbf {E} _{0}f\'\\left({\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\cdot \\mathbf {x} -c_{0}t\\right)=-{\\frac {\\partial \\mathbf {B} }{\\partial t}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6b0b7ca4cef37785746688b281008d00ab61cd74" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:41.066ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {B} ={\\frac {1}{c_{0}}}{\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\times \\mathbf {E} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msub>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mover>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">k</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">^<!-- ^ --></mo>\n</mover>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#215;<!-- &#215; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {B} ={\\frac {1}{c_{0}}}{\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\times \\mathbf {E} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {B} ={\\frac {1}{c_{0}}}{\\hat {\\mathbf {k} }}\\times \\mathbf {E} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4bff8efc4887fc7c752f3ca4cbd8bb5a30a7036" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:13.905ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The second of Maxwell\'s equations yields the magnetic field. The remaining equations will be satisfied by this choice of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} ,\\mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} ,\\mathbf {B} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {E} ,\\mathbf {B} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/96bb502fc68b1ff4b386faf29f284537f62bef7e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.692ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>.\n</p><p>The electric and magnetic field waves in the far-field travel at the speed of light. They have a special restricted orientation and proportional magnitudes, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle E_{0}=c_{0}B_{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>E</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle E_{0}=c_{0}B_{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="E_{0}=c_{0}B_{0}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e5a050c2240611ccc68f32ffd8833a88c5907742" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:10.747ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, which can be seen immediately from the <a href="/wiki/Poynting_vector" title="Poynting vector">Poynting vector</a>. The electric field, magnetic field, and direction of wave propagation are all orthogonal, and the wave propagates in the same direction as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} \\times \\mathbf {B} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">E</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#215;<!-- &#215; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi mathvariant="bold">B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mathbf {E} \\times \\mathbf {B} }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\mathbf {E} \\times \\mathbf {B} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/117c8debce39b5b81f75d132abaa52acac189c3c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.498ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. Also, <b>E</b> and <b>B</b> far-fields in free space, which as wave solutions depend primarily on these two Maxwell equations, are in-phase with each other. This is guaranteed since the generic wave solution is first order in both space and time, and the <a href="/wiki/Curl_(mathematics)" title="Curl (mathematics)">curl operator</a> on one side of these equations results in first-order spatial derivatives of the wave solution, while the time-derivative on the other side of the equations, which gives the other field, is first-order in time, resulting in the same <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phase_shift" title="Phase shift">phase shift</a> for both fields in each mathematical operation.\n</p><p>From the viewpoint of an electromagnetic wave traveling forward, the electric field might be oscillating up and down, while the magnetic field oscillates right and left. This picture can be rotated with the electric field oscillating right and left and the magnetic field oscillating down and up. This is a different solution that is traveling in the same direction. This arbitrariness in the orientation with respect to propagation direction is known as <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a>. On a quantum level, it is described as <a href="/wiki/Photon_polarization" title="Photon polarization">photon polarization</a>. The direction of the polarization is defined as the direction of the electric field.\n</p><p>More general forms of the second-order wave equations given above are available, allowing for both non-vacuum propagation media and sources. Many competing derivations exist, all with varying levels of approximation and intended applications. One very general example is a form of the electric field equation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kinsler2010_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-kinsler2010-52">[52]</a></sup> which was factorized into a pair of explicitly directional wave equations, and then efficiently reduced into a single uni-directional wave equation by means of a simple slow-evolution approximation.\n</p>\n<h2><span With UVB, the damage to DNA becomes direct, with <a href="/wiki/Photochemistry" title="Photochemistry">photochemical</a> formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrimidine_dimers" title="Pyrimidine dimers">pyrimidine dimers</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Narayanan, DL; Saladi, RN; Fox, JL (September 2010).
